<tr style="background:#f8eaba; border-top:2px solid transparent;">

<td style="width:40%; max-height:1px; border-top:2px solid transparent; border-right:1px solid transparent;">
</td>
<td style="width:60%; max-height:1px; border-top:2px solid transparent; border-left:1px solid transparent;">
</td></tr>
</tbody></table>
<p><b>Glucose</b> (also called <b>dextrose</b>) is a simple <a href="/wiki/Sugar" title="Sugar">sugar</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Chemical_formula#Molecular_formula" title="Chemical formula">molecular formula</a> <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">C</a><sub>6</sub><a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">H</a><sub>12</sub><a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">O</a><sub>6</sub><sub></sub><sub></sub><sub></sub><sub></sub><sub></sub><sub></sub><sub></sub><sub></sub><sub></sub><sub></sub><sub></sub><sub></sub><sub></sub><sub></sub><sub></sub><sub></sub><sub></sub>. Glucose is the most abundant <a href="/wiki/Monosaccharide" title="Monosaccharide">monosaccharide</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-DombKost1998_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DombKost1998-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> a subcategory of <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrates" class="mw-redirect" title="Carbohydrates">carbohydrates</a>. Glucose is mainly made by <a href="/wiki/Plants" class="mw-redirect" title="Plants">plants</a> and most <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">algae</a> during <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a> from water and carbon dioxide, using energy from sunlight. There it is used to make <a href="/wiki/Cellulose" title="Cellulose">cellulose</a> in <a href="/wiki/Cell_wall" title="Cell wall">cell walls</a>, which is the most abundant carbohydrate.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Energy_metabolism" class="mw-redirect" title="Energy metabolism">energy metabolism</a>, glucose is the most important source of energy in all <a href="/wiki/Organism" title="Organism">organisms</a>. Glucose for metabolism is partially stored as a <a href="/wiki/Polymer" title="Polymer">polymer</a>, in plants mainly as <a href="/wiki/Starch" title="Starch">starch</a> and <a href="/wiki/Amylopectin" title="Amylopectin">amylopectin</a> and in animals as <a href="/wiki/Glycogen" title="Glycogen">glycogen</a>. Glucose circulates in the blood of animals as <a href="/wiki/Blood_sugar" class="mw-redirect" title="Blood sugar">blood sugar</a>. The naturally occurring form of glucose is D-glucose, while <a href="/wiki/L-glucose" class="mw-redirect" title="L-glucose">L-glucose</a> is produced synthetically in comparably small amounts and is of lesser importance.
</p><p>Glucose is on the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization%27s_List_of_Essential_Medicines" class="mw-redirect" title="World Health Organization&#39;s List of Essential Medicines">World Health Organization's List of Essential Medicines</a>, the most important medications needed in a basic <a href="/wiki/Health_system" title="Health system">health system</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> The name glucose derives through the French from the <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> γλυκός, which means "sweet," in reference to <a href="/wiki/Must" title="Must">must</a>, the sweet, first press of grapes in the making of <a href="/wiki/Wine" title="Wine">wine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> The suffix "<a href="/wiki/-ose" title="-ose">-ose</a>" is a chemical classifier, denoting a carbohydrate.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glucose&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Glucose was first isolated from <a href="/wiki/Raisin" title="Raisin">raisins</a> in 1747 by the German chemist <a href="/wiki/Andreas_Sigismund_Marggraf" title="Andreas Sigismund Marggraf">Andreas Marggraf</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Encyclopedia_of_Food_and_Health_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclopedia_of_Food_and_Health-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Glucose was discovered in grapes by <a href="/w/index.php?title=Johann_Tobias_Lowitz&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Johann Tobias Lowitz (page does not exist)">Johann Tobias Lowitz</a> in 1792 and recognized as different from cane sugar (sucrose). Glucose is the term coined by <a href="/wiki/Jean_Baptiste_Dumas" class="mw-redirect" title="Jean Baptiste Dumas">Jean Baptiste Dumas</a> in 1838, which has prevailed in the chemical literature. <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_August_Kekul%C3%A9" class="mw-redirect" title="Friedrich August Kekulé">Friedrich August Kekulé</a> proposed the term dextrose (from Latin dexter = right), because in aqueous solution of glucose, the plane of linearly polarized light is turned to the right. In contrast, D-fructose (a ketohexose) and L-glucose turn linearly polarized light to the left. The earlier notation according to the rotation of the plane of linearly polarized light (<i>d</i> and <i>l</i>-nomenclature) was later abandoned in favor of the D- and L-notation, which refers to the absolute configuration of the asymmetric center farthest from the carbonyl group, and in concordance with the configuration of D- or L-glyceraldehyde.<sup id="cite_ref-Robyt_7_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Robyt_7-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rosanoff_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rosanoff-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Since glucose is a basic necessity of many organisms, a correct understanding of its chemical makeup and structure contributed greatly to a general advancement in <a href="/wiki/Organic_chemistry" title="Organic chemistry">organic chemistry</a>. This understanding occurred largely as a result of the investigations of <a href="/wiki/Hermann_Emil_Fischer" class="mw-redirect" title="Hermann Emil Fischer">Emil Fischer</a>, a German chemist who received the 1902 <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Chemistry" title="Nobel Prize in Chemistry">Nobel Prize in Chemistry</a> for his findings.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>  The synthesis of glucose established the structure of organic material and consequently formed the first definitive validation of <a href="/wiki/Jacobus_Henricus_van_%27t_Hoff" title="Jacobus Henricus van &#39;t Hoff">Jacobus Henricus van 't Hoff</a>'s theories of chemical kinetics and the arrangements of chemical bonds in carbon-bearing molecules.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Between 1891 and 1894, Fischer established the stereochemical configuration of all the known sugars and correctly predicted the possible <a href="/wiki/Isomer" title="Isomer">isomers</a>, applying van 't Hoff's theory of asymmetrical carbon atoms. The names initially referred to the natural substances. Their enantiomers were given the same name with the introduction of systematic nomenclatures, taking into account absolute stereochemistry (e.g. Fischer nomenclature, D/L nomenclature).
</p><p>For the discovery of the metabolism of glucose <a href="/wiki/Otto_Meyerhof" class="mw-redirect" title="Otto Meyerhof">Otto Meyerhof</a> received the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Physiology_or_Medicine" title="Nobel Prize in Physiology or Medicine">Nobel Prize in Physiology or Medicine</a> in 1922.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hans_von_Euler-Chelpin" title="Hans von Euler-Chelpin">Hans von Euler-Chelpin</a> was awarded the Nobel Prize in Chemistry along with <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Harden" title="Arthur Harden">Arthur Harden</a> in 1929 for their "research on the fermentation of sugar and their share of enzymes in this process".<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> In 1947, <a href="/wiki/Bernardo_Houssay" title="Bernardo Houssay">Bernardo Houssay</a> (for his discovery of the role of the pituitary gland in the metabolism of glucose and the derived carbohydrates) as well as <a href="/wiki/Carl_Cori" class="mw-redirect" title="Carl Cori">Carl</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gerty_Cori" title="Gerty Cori">Gerty Cori</a> (for their discovery of the conversion of glycogen from glucose) received the Nobel Prize in Physiology or Medicine.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> In 1970, <a href="/wiki/Luis_Leloir" class="mw-redirect" title="Luis Leloir">Luis Leloir</a> was awarded the Nobel Prize in Chemistry for the discovery of glucose-derived sugar nucleotides in the biosynthesis of carbohydrates.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical_properties">Chemical properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glucose&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Chemical properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>With six carbon atoms, it is classed as a <a href="/wiki/Hexose" title="Hexose">hexose</a>, a subcategory of the monosaccharides. <small>D</small>-Glucose is one of the sixteen <a href="/wiki/Aldohexose" title="Aldohexose">aldohexose</a> <a href="/wiki/Stereoisomer" class="mw-redirect" title="Stereoisomer">stereoisomers</a>. The <small>D</small>-<a href="/wiki/Isomer" title="Isomer">isomer</a>, <small>D</small>-glucose, also known as dextrose, occurs widely in nature, but the <small>L</small>-isomer, <a href="/wiki/L-glucose" class="mw-redirect" title="L-glucose"><small>L</small>-glucose</a>, does not. Glucose can be obtained by <a href="/wiki/Hydrolysis" title="Hydrolysis">hydrolysis</a> of carbohydrates such as milk sugar (<a href="/wiki/Lactose" title="Lactose">lactose</a>), cane sugar (<a href="/wiki/Sucrose" title="Sucrose">sucrose</a>), <a href="/wiki/Maltose" title="Maltose">maltose</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cellulose" title="Cellulose">cellulose</a>, <a href="/wiki/Glycogen" title="Glycogen">glycogen</a>, etc. It is commonly commercially manufactured from cornstarch by hydrolysis via pressurized steaming at controlled <a href="/wiki/PH" title="PH">pH</a> in a jet followed by further enzymatic depolymerization.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Unbonded glucose is one of the main ingredients of <a href="/wiki/Honey" title="Honey">honey</a>. All forms of glucose are colorless and easily soluble in water, <a href="/wiki/Acetic_acid" title="Acetic acid">acetic acid</a>, and several other solvents.  They are only sparingly soluble in <a href="/wiki/Methanol" title="Methanol">methanol</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">ethanol</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Structure_and_nomenclature">Structure and nomenclature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glucose&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Structure and nomenclature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Glucose is a monosaccharide with formula C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>12</sub>O<sub>6</sub> or H-(C=O)-(CHOH)<sub>5</sub>-H, whose five <a href="/wiki/Hydroxyl" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydroxyl">hydroxyl</a> (OH) groups are arranged in a specific way along its six-<a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a> back. Glucose is usually present in solid form as a <a href="/wiki/Monohydrate" class="mw-redirect" title="Monohydrate">monohydrate</a> with a closed <a href="/wiki/Pyran" title="Pyran">pyran</a> ring (dextrose hydrate). In aqueous solution, on the other hand, it is has an open-chain to a small extent and is present predominantly as α- or β-<a href="/wiki/Pyranose" title="Pyranose">pyranose</a>, which partially mutually merge by <a href="/wiki/Mutarotation" title="Mutarotation">mutarotation</a>. From aqueous solutions, the three known forms can be crystallized: α-glucopyranose, β-glucopyranose and β-glucopyranose hydrate.<sup id="cite_ref-Ullmann_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Glucose is a building block of the disaccharides <a href="/wiki/Lactose" title="Lactose">lactose</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sucrose" title="Sucrose">sucrose</a> (cane or beet sugar), of <a href="/wiki/Oligosaccharide" title="Oligosaccharide">oligosaccharides</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Raffinose" title="Raffinose">raffinose</a> and of polysaccharides such as <a href="/wiki/Starch" title="Starch">starch</a> and <a href="/wiki/Amylopectin" title="Amylopectin">amylopectin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Glycogen" title="Glycogen">glycogen</a> or <a href="/wiki/Cellulose" title="Cellulose">cellulose</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Glass_transition_temperature" class="mw-redirect" title="Glass transition temperature">glass transition temperature</a> of glucose is 31&#160;°C and the Gordon-Taylor constant (an experimentally determined constant for the prediction of the glass transition temperature for different mass fractions of a mixture of two substances)<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> is 4.5.<sup id="cite_ref-Caballero_1_76_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Caballero_1_76-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Open-chain_form">Open-chain form</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glucose&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Open-chain form">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Glucose_Fisher_to_Haworth.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Glucose_Fisher_to_Haworth.gif/220px-Glucose_Fisher_to_Haworth.gif" width="220" height="172" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/af/Glucose_Fisher_to_Haworth.gif 1.5x" data-file-width="231" data-file-height="181" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Glucose_Fisher_to_Haworth.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Glucose can exist in both a straight-chain and ring form.</div></div></div>
<p>In its fleeting <a href="/wiki/Open-chain" class="mw-redirect" title="Open-chain">open-chain</a> form, the glucose molecule has an open (as opposed to <a href="/wiki/Cyclic_compound" title="Cyclic compound">cyclic</a>) and unbranched backbone of six carbon atoms, C-1 through C-6; where C-1 is part of an <a href="/wiki/Aldehyde_group" class="mw-redirect" title="Aldehyde group">aldehyde group</a> H(C=O)-, and each of the other five carbons bears one hydroxyl group -OH.  The remaining  <a href="/wiki/Covalent_bond" title="Covalent bond">bonds</a> of the backbone carbons are satisfied by <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> atoms -H. Therefore, glucose is both a <a href="/wiki/Hexose" title="Hexose">hexose</a> and an <a href="/wiki/Aldose" title="Aldose">aldose</a>, or an <a href="/wiki/Aldohexose" title="Aldohexose">aldohexose</a>. The aldehyde group makes glucose a <a href="/wiki/Reducing_sugar" title="Reducing sugar">reducing sugar</a> giving a positive reaction with the <a href="/wiki/Fehling_test" class="mw-redirect" title="Fehling test">Fehling test</a>.
</p><p>Each of the four carbons C-2 through C-5 is a <a href="/wiki/Stereocenter" title="Stereocenter">stereocenter</a>, meaning that its four bonds connect to four different substituents.  (Carbon C-2, for example, connects to -(C=O)H, -OH, -H, and -(CHOH)<sub>4</sub>H.)  In <small>D</small>-glucose, these four parts must be in a specific three-dimensional arrangement. Namely, when the molecule is drawn in the <a href="/wiki/Fischer_projection" title="Fischer projection">Fischer projection</a>, the hydroxyls on C-2, C-4, and C-5 must be on the right side, while that on C-3 must be on the left side.
</p><p>The positions of those four hydroxyls are exactly reversed in the Fischer diagram of <a href="/wiki/L-Glucose" title="L-Glucose"><small>L</small>-glucose</a>.  <small>D</small>- and <small>L</small>-glucose are two of the 16 possible aldohexoses; the other 14 are <a href="/wiki/Allose" title="Allose">allose</a>, <a href="/wiki/Altrose" title="Altrose">altrose</a>, <a href="/wiki/Galactose" title="Galactose">galactose</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gulose" title="Gulose">gulose</a>, <a href="/wiki/Idose" title="Idose">idose</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mannose" title="Mannose">mannose</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Talose" title="Talose">talose</a>, each with two <a href="/wiki/Enantiomer" title="Enantiomer">enantiomers</a>, “<small>D</small>-” and “<small>L</small>-”.
</p><p>It is important to note that the linear form of glucose makes up less than 0.02&#160;% of the glucose molecules in a water solution. The rest is one of two cyclic forms of glucose that are formed when the hydroxyl group on carbon 5 (C5) bonds to the aldehyde carbon 1 (C1).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cyclic_forms">Cyclic forms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glucose&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Cyclic forms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

href="/wiki/File:Beta-D-Glucofuranose_Molek%C3%BClbaukasten_9136_(crop).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Beta-D-Glucofuranose_Molek%C3%BClbaukasten_9136_%28crop%29.jpg/237px-Beta-D-Glucofuranose_Molek%C3%BClbaukasten_9136_%28crop%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Beta-D-Glucofuranose_Molek%C3%BClbaukasten_9136_%28crop%29.jpg/316px-Beta-D-Glucofuranose_Molek%C3%BClbaukasten_9136_%28crop%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3089" data-file-height="2873" /></a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent"><br />From left to right: <a href="/wiki/Haworth_projection" title="Haworth projection">Haworth projections</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ball-and-stick_model" title="Ball-and-stick model">ball-and-stick</a> structures of the α- and β- <a href="/wiki/Anomer" title="Anomer">anomers</a> of <span style="font-size:85%;">D</span>-glucopyranose (top row) and <span style="font-size:85%;">D</span>-glucofuranose (bottom row)</div></div></div>
<p>In solutions, the open-chain form of glucose (either "<span style="font-size:85%;">D</span>-" or "<span style="font-size:85%;">L</span>-") exists in equilibrium with several <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate#Ring-straight_chain_isomerism" title="Carbohydrate">cyclic isomers</a>, each containing a ring of carbons closed by one oxygen atom. In aqueous solution however, more than 99% of glucose molecules, at any given time, exist as <a href="/wiki/Pyranose" title="Pyranose">pyranose</a> forms. The open-chain form is limited to about 0.25% and <a href="/wiki/Furanose" title="Furanose">furanose</a> forms exists in negligible amounts. The terms "glucose" and "<span style="font-size:85%;">D</span>-glucose" are generally used for these cyclic forms as well. The ring arises from the open-chain form by an intramolecular <a href="/wiki/Nucleophilic_addition" title="Nucleophilic addition">nucleophilic addition</a> reaction between the aldehyde group (at C-1) and either the C-4 or C-5 hydroxyl group, forming a <a href="/wiki/Hemiacetal" title="Hemiacetal">hemiacetal</a> linkage, -C(OH)H-O-.
</p><p>The reaction between C-1 and C-5 yields a six-membered <a href="/wiki/Heterocycle" class="mw-redirect" title="Heterocycle">heterocyclic</a> system called a pyranose, which is a monosaccharide sugar (hence "–ose") containing a derivatised <a href="/wiki/Pyran" title="Pyran">pyran</a> skeleton. The (much rarer) reaction between C-1 and C-4 yields a five-membered furanose ring, named after the cyclic ether <a href="/wiki/Furan" title="Furan">furan</a>.  In either case, each carbon in the ring has one hydrogen and one hydroxyl attached, except for the last carbon (C-4 or C-5) where the hydroxyl is replaced by the remainder of the open molecule (which is -(C(CH<sub>2</sub>OH)HOH)-H or -(CHOH)-H, respectively).
</p><p>The ring-closing reaction makes carbon C-1 <a href="/wiki/Chirality" title="Chirality">chiral</a>, too, since its four bonds lead to -H, to -OH, to carbon C-2, and to the ring oxygen.  These four parts of the molecule may be arranged around C-1 (the <a href="/wiki/Anomeric_carbon" class="mw-redirect" title="Anomeric carbon">anomeric carbon</a>) in two distinct ways, designated by the prefixes "α-" and "β-".  When a glucopyranose molecule is drawn in the <a href="/wiki/Haworth_projection" title="Haworth projection">Haworth projection</a>, the designation "α-" means that the hydroxyl group attached to C-1 and the -CH<sub>2</sub>OH group at C-5 lies on opposite sides of the ring's plane (a <a href="/wiki/Cis-trans_isomerism" class="mw-redirect" title="Cis-trans isomerism"><i>trans</i></a> arrangement), while "β-" means that they are on the same side of the plane (a <a href="/wiki/Cis-trans_isomerism" class="mw-redirect" title="Cis-trans isomerism"><i>cis</i></a> arrangement). Therefore, the open-chain isomer <span style="font-size:85%;">D</span>-glucose gives rise to four distinct cyclic isomers: α-<span style="font-size:85%;">D</span>-glucopyranose, β-<span style="font-size:85%;">D</span>-glucopyranose, α-<span style="font-size:85%;">D</span>-glucofuranose, and β-<span style="font-size:85%;">D</span>-glucofuranose. These five structures exist in equilibrium and interconvert, and the interconversion is much more rapid with acid <a href="/wiki/Catalysis" title="Catalysis">catalysis</a>.
</p>
<div class="center"><div class="floatnone"><a href="/wiki/File:%CE%91-d-glucose_and_%CE%B2-d-glucose_acid-catalyzed_mechanism.png" class="image" title="Widely proposed arrow-pushing mechanism for acid-catalyzed dynamic equilibrium between the α- and β- anomers of D-glucopyranose"><img alt="Widely proposed arrow-pushing mechanism for acid-catalyzed dynamic equilibrium between the α- and β- anomers of D-glucopyranose" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/%CE%91-d-glucose_and_%CE%B2-d-glucose_acid-catalyzed_mechanism.png/800px-%CE%91-d-glucose_and_%CE%B2-d-glucose_acid-catalyzed_mechanism.png" width="800" height="427" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b5/%CE%91-d-glucose_and_%CE%B2-d-glucose_acid-catalyzed_mechanism.png 1.5x" data-file-width="919" data-file-height="491" /></a></div></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright" style="background-color:white"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:392px;max-width:392px;background-color:white"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;background-color:white;margin:1px;width:165px;max-width:165px"><div class="thumbimage" style="border:none;;height:120px;overflow:hidden"><a href="/wiki/File:ALPHA-D-Glucopyranose_V.1.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/ALPHA-D-Glucopyranose_V.1.png/163px-ALPHA-D-Glucopyranose_V.1.png" width="163" height="121" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/ALPHA-D-Glucopyranose_V.1.png/245px-ALPHA-D-Glucopyranose_V.1.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/ALPHA-D-Glucopyranose_V.1.png/326px-ALPHA-D-Glucopyranose_V.1.png 2x" data-file-width="2240" data-file-height="1662" /></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;background-color:white;margin:1px;width:223px;max-width:223px"><div class="thumbimage" style="border:none;;height:120px;overflow:hidden"><a href="/wiki/File:BETA-D-Glucopyranose_V.1.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/BETA-D-Glucopyranose_V.1.png/221px-BETA-D-Glucopyranose_V.1.png" width="221" height="120" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/BETA-D-Glucopyranose_V.1.png/332px-BETA-D-Glucopyranose_V.1.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/BETA-D-Glucopyranose_V.1.png/442px-BETA-D-Glucopyranose_V.1.png 2x" data-file-width="2608" data-file-height="1420" /></a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent"><br /><a href="/wiki/Chair_conformation" class="mw-redirect" title="Chair conformation">Chair conformations</a> of α- (left) and β- (right) <span style="font-size:85%;">D</span>-glucopyranose</div></div></div>
<p>The other open-chain isomer <span style="font-size:85%;">L</span>-glucose similarly gives rise to four distinct cyclic forms of <span style="font-size:85%;">L</span>-glucose, each the mirror image of the corresponding <span style="font-size:85%;">D</span>-glucose.
</p><p>The rings are not planar, but are twisted in three dimensions. The glucopyranose ring (α or β) can assume several non-planar shapes, analogous to the "chair" and "boat" conformations of <a href="/wiki/Cyclohexane" title="Cyclohexane">cyclohexane</a>.  Similarly, the glucofuranose ring may assume several shapes, analogous to the "envelope" conformations of <a href="/wiki/Cyclopentane" title="Cyclopentane">cyclopentane</a>.
</p><p>In the solid state, only the glucopyranose forms are observed, forming colorless crystalline solids that are highly <a href="/wiki/Solubility" title="Solubility">soluble</a> in water and <a href="/wiki/Acetic_acid" title="Acetic acid">acetic acid</a> but poorly soluble in <a href="/wiki/Methanol" title="Methanol">methanol</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">ethanol</a>.  They melt at 146&#160;°C (295&#160;°F) (<i>α</i>) and 150&#160;°C (302&#160;°F) (<i>β</i>), and <a href="/wiki/Decomposition" title="Decomposition">decompose</a> at higher temperatures into <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a> and water.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rotational_isomers">Rotational isomers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glucose&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Rotational isomers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Each glucose <a href="/wiki/Isomer" title="Isomer">isomer</a> is subject to <a href="/wiki/Rotamer" class="mw-redirect" title="Rotamer">rotational isomerism</a>. Within the cyclic form of glucose, rotation may occur around the O6-C6-C5-O5 <a href="/wiki/Torsion_angle" class="mw-redirect" title="Torsion angle">torsion angle</a>, termed the <i>ω</i>-angle, to form three staggered rotamer conformations called <i>gauche</i>-<i>gauche</i> (gg), <i>gauche</i>-<i>trans</i> (gt) and <i>trans</i>-<i>gauche</i> (tg).<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> There is a tendency for the <i>ω</i>-angle to adopt a <i>gauche</i> conformation, a tendency that is attributed to the <a href="/wiki/Gauche_effect" title="Gauche effect">gauche effect</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mutarotation">Mutarotation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glucose&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Mutarotation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:442px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mutarotation_D-Glucose_V.1.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Mutarotation_D-Glucose_V.1.png/440px-Mutarotation_D-Glucose_V.1.png" width="440" height="97" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Mutarotation_D-Glucose_V.1.png/660px-Mutarotation_D-Glucose_V.1.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Mutarotation_D-Glucose_V.1.png/880px-Mutarotation_D-Glucose_V.1.png 2x" data-file-width="3153" data-file-height="697" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mutarotation_D-Glucose_V.1.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mutarotation: D-glucose molecules exist as cyclic hemiacetals that are epimeric (= diastereomeric) to each other. The epimeric ratio α:β is 36:64. In the α-D-glucopyranose (left), the blue-labeled hydroxy group is in the axial position at the anomeric center, whereas in the β-D-glucopyranose (right) the blue-labeled hydroxy group is in equatorial position at the anomeric center.</div></div></div>
<p>Mutarotation consists of a temporary reversal of the ring-forming reaction, resulting in the open-chain form, followed by a reforming of the ring. The ring closure step may use a different -OH group than the one recreated by the opening step (thus switching between pyranose and furanose forms), or the new hemiacetal group created on C-1 may have the same or opposite handedness as the original one (thus switching between the α and β forms). Thus, though the open-chain form is barely detectable in solution, it is an essential component of the equilibrium.
</p><p>The open-chain form is <a href="/wiki/Chemical_stability" title="Chemical stability">thermodynamically unstable</a>, and it spontaneously <a href="/wiki/Isomer" title="Isomer">isomerizes</a> to the cyclic forms.  (Although the ring closure reaction could in theory create four- or three-atom rings, these would be highly strained, and are not observed in practice.)  In solutions at <a href="/wiki/Room_temperature" title="Room temperature">room temperature</a>, the four cyclic isomers interconvert over a time scale of hours, in a process called <a href="/wiki/Mutarotation" title="Mutarotation">mutarotation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>  Starting from any proportions, the mixture converges to a stable ratio of α:β 36:64. The ratio would be α:β 11:89 if it were not for the influence of the <a href="/wiki/Anomeric_effect" title="Anomeric effect">anomeric effect</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>  Mutarotation is considerably slower at temperatures close to 0&#160;°C (32&#160;°F).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Optical_activity">Optical activity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glucose&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Optical activity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Whether in water or in the solid form, <small>D</small>-(+)-glucose is <a href="/wiki/Dextrorotation_and_levorotation" title="Dextrorotation and levorotation">dextrorotatory</a>, meaning it will rotate the direction of <a href="/wiki/Polarized_light" class="mw-redirect" title="Polarized light">polarized light</a> clockwise as seen looking toward the light source.  The effect is due to the <a href="/wiki/Chirality" title="Chirality">chirality</a> of the molecules, and indeed the mirror-image isomer, <small>L</small>-(−)-glucose, is <a href="/wiki/Dextrorotation_and_levorotation" title="Dextrorotation and levorotation">levorotatory</a> (rotates polarized light counterclockwise) by the same amount. The strength of the effect is different for each of the five <a href="/wiki/Tautomer" title="Tautomer">tautomers</a>.
</p><p>Note that the <small>D</small>- prefix does not refer directly to the optical properties of the compound. It indicates that the C-5 chiral center has the same handedness as that of <small>D</small>-glyceraldehyde (which was so labeled because it is dextrorotatory).  The fact that <small>D</small>-glucose is dextrorotatory is a combined effect of its four chiral centers, not just of C-5; and indeed some of the other <small>D</small>-aldohexoses are levorotatory.
</p><p>The conversion between the two anomers can be observed in a <a href="/wiki/Polarimeter" title="Polarimeter">polarimeter</a>, since pure α- D- glucose has a specific rotation angle of +112.2 ° · ml · dm<sup>−1</sup> · g<sup>−1</sup>, pure β- D- glucose of +17.5 ° · ml · dm<sup>−1</sup> · g<sup>-1</sup>.<sup id="cite_ref-Hesse_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hesse-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> When equilibrium has been reached after a certain time due to mutarotation, the angle of rotation is +52.7 ° · ml · dm<sup>-1</sup> · g<sup>-1</sup>.<sup id="cite_ref-Hesse_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hesse-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> By adding acid or base, this transformation is much accelerated. The equilibration takes place via the open-chain aldehyde form.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Isomerisation">Isomerisation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glucose&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Isomerisation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In dilute <a href="/wiki/Sodium_hydroxide" title="Sodium hydroxide">sodium hydroxide</a> or other dilute bases, the monosaccharides <a href="/wiki/Mannose" title="Mannose">mannose</a>, glucose and <a href="/wiki/Fructose" title="Fructose">fructose</a> interconvert (via a <a href="/wiki/Lobry_de_Bruyn%E2%80%93van_Ekenstein_transformation" title="Lobry de Bruyn–van Ekenstein transformation">Lobry de Bruyn–Alberda–van Ekenstein transformation</a>), so that a balance between these isomers is formed. This reaction proceeds via an <a href="/wiki/Enediol" class="mw-redirect" title="Enediol">enediol</a>:
</p><p><a href="/wiki/File:Glucose_Fructose_Mannose_Gleichgewicht.png" class="image" title="Glucose-Fructose-Mannose-isomerisation"><img alt="Glucose-Fructose-Mannose-isomerisation" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Glucose_Fructose_Mannose_Gleichgewicht.png/440px-Glucose_Fructose_Mannose_Gleichgewicht.png" width="440" height="202" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/79/Glucose_Fructose_Mannose_Gleichgewicht.png 1.5x" data-file-width="657" data-file-height="302" /></a>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biochemical_properties">Biochemical properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glucose&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Biochemical properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="toccolours collapsible collapsed" style="width:100%; text-align:left;">

<tbody><tr>
<th>Metabolism of common <a href="/wiki/Monosaccharide" title="Monosaccharide">monosaccharides</a> and some biochemical reactions of glucose
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td><div class="floatnone"><a href="/wiki/File:Metabolism_of_common_monosaccharides,_and_related_reactions.png" class="image"><img alt="Metabolism of common monosaccharides, and related reactions.png" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Metabolism_of_common_monosaccharides%2C_and_related_reactions.png/1000px-Metabolism_of_common_monosaccharides%2C_and_related_reactions.png" width="1000" height="1213" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Metabolism_of_common_monosaccharides%2C_and_related_reactions.png/1500px-Metabolism_of_common_monosaccharides%2C_and_related_reactions.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Metabolism_of_common_monosaccharides%2C_and_related_reactions.png/2000px-Metabolism_of_common_monosaccharides%2C_and_related_reactions.png 2x" data-file-width="2785" data-file-height="3379" /></a></div>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Glucose is the most abundant monosaccharide. Glucose is also the most widely used <a href="/wiki/Aldohexose" title="Aldohexose">aldohexose</a> in most living organisms. One possible explanation for this is that glucose has a lower tendency than other aldohexoses to react nonspecifically with the <a href="/wiki/Amine" title="Amine">amine</a> groups of <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Higgins_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Higgins-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> This reaction—<a href="/wiki/Glycation" title="Glycation">glycation</a>—impairs or destroys the function of many proteins,<sup id="cite_ref-Higgins_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Higgins-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> e.g. in <a href="/wiki/Glycated_hemoglobin" title="Glycated hemoglobin">glycated hemoglobin</a>. Glucose's low rate of glycation can be attributed to its having a more stable <a href="/wiki/Glucose#Cyclic_forms" title="Glucose">cyclic form</a> compared to other aldohexoses, which means it spends less time than they do in its reactive <a href="/wiki/Glucose#Open-chain_form" title="Glucose">open-chain form</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Higgins_29-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Higgins-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> The reason for glucose having the most stable cyclic form of all the aldohexoses is that its <a href="/wiki/Hydroxy_group#Hydroxy_group" title="Hydroxy group">hydroxy groups</a> (with the exception of the hydroxy group on the anomeric carbon of <small>D</small>-glucose) are in the <a href="/wiki/Cyclohexane_conformation" title="Cyclohexane conformation">equatorial position</a>. Presumably, glucose is the most abundant natural monosaccharide because it is less glycated with proteins than other monosaccharides.<sup id="cite_ref-Higgins_29-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Higgins-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Stryer_531_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stryer_531-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Another hypothesis is that glucose, being the only <a href="/wiki/Aldohexose" title="Aldohexose">D-aldohexose</a> that has all five <a href="/wiki/Hydroxy_group" title="Hydroxy group">hydroxy</a> substituents in the <a href="/wiki/Equatorial_bond" class="mw-redirect" title="Equatorial bond">equatorial</a> position in the form of β-D-glucose, is more readily accessible to chemical reactions,<sup id="cite_ref-Garrett_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Garrett-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> for example, for <a href="/wiki/Esterification" class="mw-redirect" title="Esterification">esterification</a><sup id="cite_ref-Voet_363_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voet_363-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> or <a href="/wiki/Acetal" title="Acetal">acetal</a> formation.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> For this reason, D-glucose is also a highly preferred building block in natural <a href="/wiki/Polysaccharides" class="mw-redirect" title="Polysaccharides">polysaccharides</a> (glycans). Polysaccharides that are composed solely of Glucose are termed <a href="/wiki/Glucan" title="Glucan">glucans</a>.
</p><p>Glucose is produced by plants through the photosynthesis using sunlight, water and carbon dioxide and can be used by all living organisms as an energy and carbon source. However, most glucose does not occur in its free form, but in the form of its polymers, i.e. lactose, sucrose, starch and others which are energy reserve substances, and cellulose and <a href="/wiki/Chitin" title="Chitin">chitin</a>, which are components of the cell wall in plants or <a href="/wiki/Fungi" class="mw-redirect" title="Fungi">fungi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arthropod" title="Arthropod">arthropods</a>, respectively. These polymers are degraded to glucose during food intake by animals, fungi and bacteria using enzymes.  All animals are also able to produce glucose themselves from certain precursors as the need arises. <a href="/wiki/Nerve_cells" class="mw-redirect" title="Nerve cells">Nerve cells</a>, cells of the <a href="/wiki/Renal_medulla" title="Renal medulla">renal medulla</a> and <a href="/wiki/Erythrocytes" class="mw-redirect" title="Erythrocytes">erythrocytes</a> depend on glucose for their energy production.<sup id="cite_ref-Löffler/Petrides_195_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Löffler/Petrides_195-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> In adult humans, there are about 18 g of glucose,<sup id="cite_ref-Satyanarayana_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Satyanarayana-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> of which about 4 g are present in the blood.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Approximately 180 to 220 g of glucose are produced in the liver of an adult in 24 hours.<sup id="cite_ref-Satyanarayana_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Satyanarayana-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Many of the long-term complications of <a href="/wiki/Diabetes_mellitus" title="Diabetes mellitus">diabetes</a> (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Visual_impairment" title="Visual impairment">blindness</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kidney_failure" title="Kidney failure">kidney failure</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Peripheral_neuropathy" title="Peripheral neuropathy">peripheral neuropathy</a>) are probably due to the glycation of proteins or <a href="/wiki/Lipid" title="Lipid">lipids</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> In contrast, <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzyme</a>-regulated addition of sugars to protein is called <a href="/wiki/Glycosylation" title="Glycosylation">glycosylation</a> and is essential for the function of many proteins.<sup id="cite_ref-varki_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-varki-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Uptake">Uptake</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glucose&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Uptake">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Ingested glucose initially binds to the receptor for sweet taste on the tongue in humans. This complex of the proteins <a href="/wiki/T1R2" class="mw-redirect" title="T1R2">T1R2</a> and <a href="/wiki/T1R3" class="mw-redirect" title="T1R3">T1R3</a> makes it possible to identify glucose-containing food sources. Glucose mainly comes from food - about 300 g per day are produced by conversion of food,<sup id="cite_ref-Löffler/Petrides_404_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Löffler/Petrides_404-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> but it is also synthesized from other metabolites in the body's cells. In humans, the breakdown of glucose-containing polysaccharides happens in part already during <a href="/wiki/Mastication" class="mw-redirect" title="Mastication">chewing</a> by means of <a href="/wiki/Amylase" title="Amylase">amylase</a>, which is contained in <a href="/wiki/Saliva" title="Saliva">saliva</a>, as well as by <a href="/wiki/Maltase" title="Maltase">maltase</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lactase" title="Lactase">lactase</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sucrase" title="Sucrase">sucrase</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Brush_border" title="Brush border">brush border</a> of the small intestine. Glucose is a building block of many carbohydrates and can be split off from them using certain enzymes. <a href="/wiki/Glucosidases" title="Glucosidases">Glucosidases</a>, a subgroup of the glycosidases, first catalyze the hydrolysis of long-chain glucose-containing polysaccharides, removing terminal glucose. In turn, disaccharides are mostly degraded by specific glycosidases to glucose. The names of the degrading enzymes are often derived from the particular poly- and disaccharide; inter alia, for the degradation of polysaccharide chains there are amylases (named after amylose, a component of starch), cellulases (named after cellulose), chitinases (named after chitin) and more. Furthermore, for the cleavage of disaccharides, there are maltase, lactase, sucrase, <a href="/wiki/Trehalase" title="Trehalase">trehalase</a> and others. In humans, about 70 genes are known that code for glycosidases. They have functions in the digestion and degradation of glycogen, <a href="/wiki/Sphingolipids" class="mw-redirect" title="Sphingolipids">sphingolipids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mucopolysaccharides" class="mw-redirect" title="Mucopolysaccharides">mucopolysaccharides</a> and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Poly(ADP-ribose)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Poly(ADP-ribose) (page does not exist)">poly(ADP-ribose)</a>. Humans do not produce cellulases, chitinases and trehalases, but the bacteria in the <a href="/wiki/Gut_flora" title="Gut flora">gut flora</a> do.
</p><p>In order to get into or out of cell membranes of cells and membranes of cell compartments, glucose requires special transport proteins from the <a href="/wiki/Major_facilitator_superfamily" title="Major facilitator superfamily">major facilitator superfamily</a>. In the <a href="/wiki/Small_intestine" title="Small intestine">small intestine</a> (more precisely, in the <a href="/wiki/Jejunum" title="Jejunum">jejunum</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-Harper_641_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harper_641-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> glucose is taken up into the intestinal <a href="/wiki/Epithelial_cell" class="mw-redirect" title="Epithelial cell">epithelial cells</a> with the help of <a href="/wiki/Glucose_transporter" title="Glucose transporter">glucose transporters</a><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> via a <a href="/wiki/Secondary_active_transport" class="mw-redirect" title="Secondary active transport">secondary active transport</a> mechanism called sodium ion-glucose <a href="/wiki/Symport" class="mw-redirect" title="Symport">symport</a> via the <a href="/wiki/SGLT1" class="mw-redirect" title="SGLT1">sodium/glucose cotransporter 1</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Löffler/Petrides_199_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Löffler/Petrides_199-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> The further transfer occurs on the <a href="/wiki/Basolateral" class="mw-redirect" title="Basolateral">basolateral</a> side of the intestinal epithelial cells via the glucose transporter <a href="/wiki/GLUT2" title="GLUT2">GLUT2</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Löffler/Petrides_199_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Löffler/Petrides_199-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> as well as their uptake into <a href="/wiki/Hepatocyte" title="Hepatocyte">liver cells</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kidney_cell&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Kidney cell (page does not exist)">kidney cells</a>, cells of the <a href="/wiki/Islets_of_Langerhans" class="mw-redirect" title="Islets of Langerhans">islets of Langerhans</a>, nerve cells, <a href="/wiki/Astrocyte" title="Astrocyte">astrocytes</a> and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tanyocyte&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Tanyocyte (page does not exist)">tanyocytes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Glucose enters the liver via the <a href="/wiki/Vena_portae" class="mw-redirect" title="Vena portae">vena portae</a> and is stored there as a cellular glycogen.<sup id="cite_ref-Löffler/Petrides_214_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Löffler/Petrides_214-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> In the liver cell, it is <a href="/wiki/Phosphorylation" title="Phosphorylation">phosphorylated</a> by <a href="/wiki/Glucokinase" title="Glucokinase">glucokinase</a> at position 6 to <a href="/wiki/Glucose-6-phosphate" class="mw-redirect" title="Glucose-6-phosphate">glucose-6-phosphate</a>, which can not leave the cell. With the help of <a href="/wiki/Glucose-6-phosphatase" class="mw-redirect" title="Glucose-6-phosphatase">glucose-6-phosphatase</a>, glucose-6-phosphate is converted back into glucose exclusively in the liver, if necessary, so that it is available for maintaining a sufficient blood glucose concentration. In other cells, uptake happens by passive transport through one of the 14 GLUT proteins.<sup id="cite_ref-Löffler/Petrides_199_42-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Löffler/Petrides_199-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> In the other cell types, phosphorylation occurs through a <a href="/wiki/Hexokinase" title="Hexokinase">hexokinase</a>, whereupon glucose can no longer diffuse out of the cell.
</p><p>The glucose transporter <a href="/wiki/GLUT1" title="GLUT1">GLUT1</a> is produced by most cell types and is of particular importance for nerve cells and pancreatic <a href="/wiki/Beta_cell" title="Beta cell">β-cells</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Löffler/Petrides_199_42-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Löffler/Petrides_199-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/GLUT3" title="GLUT3">GLUT3</a> is highly expressed in nerve cells.<sup id="cite_ref-Löffler/Petrides_199_42-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Löffler/Petrides_199-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Glucose from the bloodstream is taken up by <a href="/wiki/GLUT4" title="GLUT4">GLUT4</a> from <a href="/wiki/Muscle_cells" class="mw-redirect" title="Muscle cells">muscle cells</a> (of the <a href="/wiki/Skeletal_muscle" title="Skeletal muscle">skeletal muscle</a><sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Heart_muscle" class="mw-redirect" title="Heart muscle">heart muscle</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Fat_cell" class="mw-redirect" title="Fat cell">fat cells</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/w/index.php?title=GLUT14&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="GLUT14 (page does not exist)">GLUT14</a> is formed exclusively in <a href="/wiki/Testes" class="mw-redirect" title="Testes">testes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Löffler/Petrides_199_42-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Löffler/Petrides_199-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Excess glucose is broken down and converted into fatty acids, which are stored as <a href="/wiki/Triacylglyceride" class="mw-redirect" title="Triacylglyceride">triacylglycerides</a>. In the <a href="/wiki/Kidney" title="Kidney">kidneys</a>, glucose in the urine is absorbed via SGLT1 and <a href="/wiki/SGLT2" title="SGLT2">SGLT2</a> in the apical cell membranes and transmitted via <a href="/wiki/GLUT2" title="GLUT2">GLUT2</a> in the basolateral cell membranes.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> About 90% of kidney glucose reabsorption is via SGLT2 and about 3% via SGLT1.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biosynthesis">Biosynthesis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glucose&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Biosynthesis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Gluconeogenesis" title="Gluconeogenesis">Gluconeogenesis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Glycogenolysis" title="Glycogenolysis">Glycogenolysis</a></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Plant" title="Plant">plants</a> and some <a href="/wiki/Prokaryote" title="Prokaryote">prokaryotes</a>, glucose is a product of <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> Glucose is also formed by the breakdown of polymeric forms of glucose like <a href="/wiki/Glycogen" title="Glycogen">glycogen</a> (in animals and <a href="/wiki/Mushroom" title="Mushroom">mushrooms</a>) or <a href="/wiki/Starch" title="Starch">starch</a> (in plants). The cleavage of glycogen is termed <a href="/wiki/Glycogenolysis" title="Glycogenolysis">glycogenolysis</a>, the cleavage of starch is called starch degradation.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The metabolic pathway that begins with molecules containing two to four carbon atoms (C) and ends in the glucose molecule containing six carbon atoms is called gluconeogenesis and occurs in all living organisms. The smaller starting materials are the result of other metabolic pathways. Ultimately almost all <a href="/wiki/Biomolecule" title="Biomolecule">biomolecules</a> come from the assimilation of carbon dioxide in plants during photosynthesis.<sup id="cite_ref-Voet_359_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voet_359-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> The free energy of formation of α-D-glucose is 917.2 kilojoules per mole.<sup id="cite_ref-Voet_59_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voet_59-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> In humans, gluconeogenesis occurs in the liver and kidney,<sup id="cite_ref-Szablewski_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Szablewski-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> but also in other cell types. In the liver about 150 g of glycogen are stored, in skeletal muscle about 250 g.<sup id="cite_ref-Löffler/Petrides_389_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Löffler/Petrides_389-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> However, the glucose released in muscle cells upon cleavage of the glycogen can not be delivered to the circulation because glucose is phosphorylated by the hexokinae, and a glucose-6-phosphatase is not expressed to remove the phosphate group. Unlike for glucose, there is no transport protein for glucose-6-phosphate. Gluconeogenesis allows the organism to build up glucose from other metabolites, including <a href="/wiki/Lactic_acid" title="Lactic acid">lactate</a> or certain amino acids, while consuming energy. The renal <a href="/wiki/Tubular_cell" class="mw-redirect" title="Tubular cell">tubular cells</a> can also produce glucose.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Glucose_Degradation">Glucose Degradation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glucose&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Glucose Degradation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Glucose_metabolism.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Glucose_metabolism.svg/220px-Glucose_metabolism.svg.png" width="220" height="171" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Glucose_metabolism.svg/330px-Glucose_metabolism.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Glucose_metabolism.svg/440px-Glucose_metabolism.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1390" data-file-height="1080" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Glucose_metabolism.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Glucose metabolism and various forms of it in the process<br />Glucose-containing compounds and <a href="/wiki/Isomer" title="Isomer">isomeric</a> forms are digested and taken up by the body in the intestines, including <a href="/wiki/Starch" title="Starch">starch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Glycogen" title="Glycogen">glycogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Disaccharides" class="mw-redirect" title="Disaccharides">disaccharides</a> and <a href="/wiki/Monosaccharides" class="mw-redirect" title="Monosaccharides">monosaccharides</a>.<br />Glucose is stored in mainly the liver and muscles as glycogen. It is distributed and used in tissues as free glucose.</div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Glycolysis" title="Glycolysis">Glycolysis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pentose_phosphate_pathway" title="Pentose phosphate pathway">Pentose phosphate pathway</a></div>
<p>In humans, glucose is metabolised by glycolysis<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> and the pentose phosphate pathway.<sup id="cite_ref-Horton_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Horton-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Glycolysis is used by all living organisms,<sup id="cite_ref-Hall_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hall-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Garrett_551_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Garrett_551-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> with small variations, and all organisms generate energy from the breakdown of monosaccharides.<sup id="cite_ref-Hall_57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hall-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> In the further course of the metabolism, it can be completely degraded via <a href="/wiki/Oxidative_decarboxylation" title="Oxidative decarboxylation">oxidative decarboxylation</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Krebs_cycle" class="mw-redirect" title="Krebs cycle">Krebs cycle</a> (synonym <i>citric acid cycle</i>) and the <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_chain" class="mw-redirect" title="Respiratory chain">respiratory chain</a> to water and carbon dioxide. If there is not enough oxygen available for this, the glucose degradation in animals occurs anaerobic to lactate via lactic acid fermentation and releases less energy. Muscular lactate enters the liver through the bloodstream in mammals, where gluconeogenesis occurs (<a href="/wiki/Cori_cycle" title="Cori cycle">Cori cycle</a>). With a high supply of glucose, the metabolite <a href="/wiki/Acetyl-CoA" title="Acetyl-CoA">acetyl-CoA</a> from the Krebs cycle can also be used for <a href="/wiki/Fatty_acid_synthesis" title="Fatty acid synthesis">fatty acid synthesis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> Glucose is also used to replenish the body's glycogen stores, which are mainly found in liver and skeletal muscle. These processes are <a href="/wiki/Hormone" title="Hormone">hormonally</a> regulated. 
</p><p>In other living organisms, other forms of fermentation can occur. The bacterium <i><a href="/wiki/Escherichia_coli" title="Escherichia coli">Escherichia coli</a></i> can grow on nutrient media containing glucose as the sole carbon source.<sup id="cite_ref-Voet_59_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voet_59-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> In some bacteria and, in modified form, also in archaea, glucose is degraded via the <a href="/wiki/Entner-Doudoroff_pathway" class="mw-redirect" title="Entner-Doudoroff pathway">Entner-Doudoroff pathway</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Use of glucose as an energy source in cells is by either aerobic respiration, anaerobic respiration, or fermentation. The first step of glycolysis is the <a href="/wiki/Phosphorylation" title="Phosphorylation">phosphorylation</a> of glucose by a <a href="/wiki/Hexokinase" title="Hexokinase">hexokinase</a> to form <a href="/wiki/Glucose_6-phosphate" title="Glucose 6-phosphate">glucose 6-phosphate</a>. The main reason for the immediate phosphorylation of glucose is to prevent its diffusion out of the cell as the charged <a href="/wiki/Phosphate" title="Phosphate">phosphate</a> group prevents glucose 6-phosphate from easily crossing the <a href="/wiki/Cell_membrane" title="Cell membrane">cell membrane</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Furthermore, addition of the high-energy phosphate group <a href="/wiki/Activation#Biochemistry" title="Activation">activates</a> glucose for subsequent breakdown in later steps of glycolysis. At <a href="/wiki/Physiological_condition" title="Physiological condition">physiological conditions</a>, this initial reaction is irreversible.
</p><p>In anaerobic respiration, one glucose molecule produces a net gain of two ATP molecules (four ATP molecules are produced during glycolysis through substrate-level phosphorylation, but two are required by enzymes used during the process).<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> In aerobic respiration, a molecule of glucose is much more profitable in that a maximum net production of 30 or 32 ATP molecules (depending on the organism) through oxidative phosphorylation is generated.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Click on genes, proteins and metabolites below to link to respective articles.</i> <sup id="cite_ref-WikiPathways_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WikiPathways-64">&#91;§ 1&#93;</a></sup> 
</p>

<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:WP534.png" title="File:WP534.png"> </a></div>Glycolysis and Gluconeogenesis  <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.wikipathways.org/index.php/Pathway:WP534">edit</a>
